not, remaining independent initially is good to maintain your customers and
ease them into the fold of the acquirer. When Marriott bought Starwood and
PayPal bought Venmo, it was this model.
Direct competitor: your product and customer base are the same as
theirs
You are competing directly with them, so the acquirer wants your customer
base and probably wants to completely remove you as a thorn in their side.
There is no option to remain independent here; you are being assimilated by
the (hopefully nice) Borg. They will fully integrate your product into theirs,
and your customers will be quickly merged with theirs. They may not keep
all of your team if some are duplicative with theirs, and those who are kept
will be added into the appropriate teams and may not be kept all together.
This is what happened when Verisign acquired GeoTrust, Exxon acquired
Mobil, AT&T acquired Time Warner, and Disney acquired Pixar.
Acquihire: they just want your team

---

## Page 357

This type of acquisition is incredibly common in the technology sector.
Definition
Acquihire 
is a combination of the words 
acquisition
 and 
hire
. This is when a
company acquires a startup based on the team’s skills, rather than the product
or service.
The scenario is you built a stellar team, and hiring great people is hard. Your
acquirer discovered how great your team is and wants to grab them quickly
before someone else finds out how good they are. It’s typically not a barn
burner exit, for sure, but for the founders, it can be a very nice outcome.
Facebook has done this a hundred times already with companies like Drop.io,
Hot Potato, Beluga, and FriendFeed. Google did it with Aardvark, Slide,
Like.com, reMail. And Twitter did it with Summize and Fluther. In all those
cases, you will find that people from those startups ended up in very senior
roles within the big acquirer.
31.3.2 Nuts and bolts issues to nail down for you and your team
Sure, you want to get a good valuation for your company, and you need to
know what’s going to happen with things like operating capital and
indemnification. Typically, there will also be an escrow set up to deal with
potential legal actions, such as from shareholders not happy with the deal or
vendors who believe they are owed money. But it’s the stuff with respect to
you and your team that sets the tone for what happens to your company
inside the new one over the long term: Does it even exist inside after a couple
of years? Don’t trust these to just happen, and don’t forget to hammer them
out in writing in advance of the transaction closing.
Compensation
The salaries of everyone in your company, founders and management
included, have been startup salaries because everyone accepted that a big part
of their comp was in equity. Now all of your employees (this does not count
senior managers and founders who most likely have shares and not options)
are going to (hopefully) reap the benefits of those options turning into cash as

---

## Page 358

part of this acquisition (which was the point of their options in the first
place), but now they are going to be part of a bigger company where there
won’t be big payouts presumably. They now need a competitive, big-
company salary. The best way to make that happen, since the acquirer might
just keep people’s salaries the same, is for you to change their salary to that
level 
before
 the acquisition. It’s a trick most startups forget, to their team’s
detriment. You do this adjustment when you are quite sure the transaction is
going through but not so close to the closing that it draws the ire of the
transaction team. I have experienced the transaction team encouraging this
because they know the wheels of HR inside their company move very slowly.
Titles
Big companies typically have a very formal set of titles that map to levels of
compensation and authority. However, titles like senior engineer, principal
engineer, and director don’t mean the same thing from one big company to
another. You will have to get that mapping from the new HR department, and
then it will be up to you to make the mapping for your team and advocate for
your team to get the appropriate titles and to make sure their responsibility,
salary, bonus, and other compensation fits into the acquirer’s rigid structure.
Retention
During their due diligence phase, an acquirer gets to know the team they will
be inheriting. Part of this is to determine which people are key employees to
retain. They will offer these key people extra incentives to stick around for a
specified period. Try to make sure that amount of time is one year or at most
two, not the three that some acquirers try for—three years for someone who
had self-selected to be a startup person will feel like a very long time. There
is a secondary question about how to retain the people who want to take their
equity earnings and leave right away. That happening might make what you
need to do for the acquirer to be successful extra difficult. You can address
attrition by negotiating a separate retention fund you can use to try to retain
those people who don’t care if they are key, they just don’t want to stick
around very long. Even retaining your whole staff for a short time could be
very helpful to getting your product integrated into the new company.

---

## Page 359

Hiring
As someone who has also worked at very large companies, I know
controlling expenses through staffing constraints is their modus operandi.
Again, if you get an agreement up front before close as to what hiring you are
going to need to do to accomplish the goals you and they have mutually
agreed to, hiring will be possible. If you don’t get this agreement, hiring
might not be possible because you’ll find yourself in competition with senior
managers who have been at the company a long time and know the ropes.
You
Where do you fit in the new organization? It is important you know who you
report to. The more authority and seniority that person has, the better your
life will be because they are your “overseer” or your “caretaker,” especially
during some transition period, and you want them to have the clout to protect
your interests. Remember, this is a big company, complete with tons of
politics you may not be used to. Make sure you only report to one person—
many large companies these days are using matrix reporting structures.
Definition
Matrix organizations
 are structures where people have both a primary
manager and a project manager.
This happens with engineering people all the time, but I’ve seen it for other
functions as well. In my opinion, this kind of structure is a nightmare,
especially if you are new and don’t know where all the big-company politics
are going to come from. If you are a standalone business unit, it sounds good,
but the reporting structure here can be really challenging. Some founders
have negotiated a board of senior leaders, of which they were a part, to
manage their new business unit.
Earnouts
This is a situation that is typically good for the buyers and bad for the startup

---

## Page 360

team.
Definition
Earnouts
 are where compensation for the startup’s management team being
acquired is tied to hitting a set of milestones.
There are so many unknowns—much more than the uncertainty you lived
with while a startup—that they are fraught with risk to you. It may be off the
table, but taking less money up front versus more money later that is tied to
earnouts could be a good decision for you.
Culture
It is vital you understand their culture, or two years could end up feeling like
an eternity. Your team needs to feel comfortable in the new culture, so make
sure you dig into whether this is a company that reflects the values you all
have been sharing for quite some time as you built up your company. Talk to
the people at the new parent company who form the acquisition team. This
will be a cross-functional team that will be friendly because they are all
happy to be working on another acquisition. They will talk and probably
quite openly. Also, try to talk to any other small company people you can
find in the company who previously were acquired by them. What happens if
you find out the culture is bad? You can still cancel the deal if it’s that bad.
Or you continue but make sure the commitment time your people have to stay
at the company to get their compensation is as short as possible.
31.3.3 The “day after” the acquisition
One critical question to ask before the close is about the acquirer’s “day
after” plan. These are the details of how they plan to execute the integration
of your company into theirs, especially with respect to you, your employees,
and your customers. No matter what, the integration process is always
chaotic, stressful, and confusing. You will still feel like a team at first, and
your employees will look to you for explanations of what is happening. Keep
communicating steadily to keep them as comfortable as can be. Their roles
may be changing, and yours certainly will. Culture almost certainly will be a

---

## Page 361

change unless you are being acquired by another startup. They will have a lot
of systems and processes—almost certainly a 
lot more processes than what a
startup is used to. That will feel stifling. There of course will be a lot of new
people to get to know. An organization chart will be essential for you and
your team. On the good side, the acquirer wants you and will be investing in
you, and this means you may suddenly have more resources than you ever
had as a startup.
But the thing I cannot stress enough is this: being acquired is the goal of an
entrepreneur, and it is a rare and amazing accomplishment. When you’re
down in the details of the deal and the integration process, you may have
trouble seeing the forest for the trees. But step back, celebrate this
accomplishment, and patiently work hard to make the outcome for everyone
the best you can.
31.4 The moral of this anecdote
IPOs are complex, long, difficult, and very rare. For every 10 startups, 5 fail,
3 just return the capital invested in them, and 2 are the big wins that will to be
the basis of the returns of an investor’s fund. This means it is not the 10X
return that most entrepreneurs think their company needs to return; it is 30X.
An entrepreneur needs to be planning on an acquisition all the time, even
when all you can think about is making payroll, getting revenues, and
growing the business. Sure, some offers come in unsolicited, but most happen
because you have cultivated a set of relationships. When an offer comes in to
acquire, it’s at first a major gong-ringing moment. As always, the devil is in
the details, and it would be heavenly if there were multiple offers to choose
between, but that too is rare. There is a lot to think about and plan for,
starting with what type of structure the acquisition will take. Then there is the
planning for your team and making sure points relative to team are in the deal
and not left to chance. In the end, despite the exhausting amount of work
involved in being acquired, it is the outcome all entrepreneurs dream for, and
the work is well worth it.
